                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Dual+Ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20dual%20ion%20battery">sodium dual ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anodes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anodes">anodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Pre-sodiation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_pre-sodiation">pre-sodiation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Phosphorus-doped+Soft+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_phosphorus-doped%20soft%20carbon">phosphorus-doped soft carbon</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Rechargeable batteries, which could promote the shift from petrol (gasoline)-powered vehicles to electrically powered vehicles, have attracted extensive attention for decades. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> However, the further application of traditional lithium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage systems (EESs) is hindered by the high cost and limited lithium resources. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> Therefore, more and more researchers are working on alternative rechargeable batteries, such as sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 3 to 11">(3−11)</a> potassium-ion batteries, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 12 to 14">(12−14)</a> metal–sulfur batteries, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 15 to 18">(15−18)</a> and dual-ion batteries (DIBs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 19 to 31">(19−31)</a> Among the various rechargeable batteries, DIBs are considered to be the effective, low cost, and eco-friendly EESs for energy storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref24 ref27 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref24 ref27 ref32" aria-label="reference 22, 24, 27 and 32">(22,24,27,32)</a> As we know, the energy density of the whole battery could be improved by improving the voltage of the cathode or lowering the voltage of the anode. Therefore, it is important to develop advanced cathode/anode materials to meet the need of the high-energy SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 33 to 36">(33−36)</a> It is known that the anions could insert/desert into/from graphite cathodes at high voltage in DIBs, which could improve the voltage of the full cell, further leading to enhanced energy density. Moreover, the abundant and low cost graphite resources will further reduce the cost of the batteries. In addition, these merits have made the graphite a promising cathode for DIBs. In contrast to the traditional rocking-chair batteries, cations together with anions are contributing to the specific capacities of DIBs during charge/discharge processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Typically, the anions/cations in the electrolyte intercalate into cathode/anode materials during the charge process and deintercalate from cathode/anode back to the electrolyte during the discharge process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> In the past, extensive investigation of electrolytes in DIBs focused on lithium-based salts and other ionic liquids. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref37 ref39 ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref37 ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 25, 37 and 39 to 41">(25,37,39−41)</a> Until recently, the sodium-based DIBs (NDIBs) have been developed and attracted much attention because of the abundance and low cost of sodium resources. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref42" aria-label="reference 24 and 42">(24,42)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">It is known that the anodes of SIBs usually suffer from a large amount of sodium loss because of the solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation during the initial cycle, and the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of SIBs mostly is lower than 70%. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 33 and 43 to 46">(33,43−46)</a> In the sodium half cells, the influence of this trouble is unconspicuous because of the far in excess of sodium in the sodium electrode. As this problem will result in severe issues in the full cells, including low capacity, inferior cycle stability, and limited lifespan, it should be treated with caution. The NDIBs suffer from, not surprisingly, low Coulombic efficiency and inferior cycle stability as well. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref24 ref27 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref24 ref27 ref37" aria-label="reference 22, 24, 27 and 37">(22,24,27,37)</a> In addition, the low capacity of carbonaceous anode and the sodium depletion in the electrolyte would significantly reduce the capacity and energy density of NDIBs. Therefore, it is urgent to develop new anode materials with high capacity and new methods to offset the initial irreversible loss of sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In this contribution, we synthesized a high capacity phosphorus-doped soft carbon (P-SC) anode and demonstrated a valid approach to offset the initial sodium loss via anode presodiation. As a consequence, the NDIBs with the P-SC anode after presodiation exhibited improved Coulombic efficiency (&gt;90%), high discharge capacity (118 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), excellent rate performance, and long cycle stability (81 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 900 cycles at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with capacity retention of 81.8%), much superior to the unsodiated NDIBs (u-NDIBs), which delivers poor Coulombic efficiency of 75% and inferior cycle stability (44 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention of 47.8%).</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Pyrolysis of perylene tetracarboxylic dianhydride (PTCDA) as a feasible method to obtain soft carbon (SC) has been reported. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref49 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 42, 49 and 50">(42,49,50)</a>    PTCDA  was purchased from J&amp;K Scientific, and used as received. The P-SC and SC were obtained from thermal polymerization of PTCDA with a slow heating rate. Typically, in order to obtain the P-SC, 2 g PTCDA and 2 g phosphorus powder were homogeneously mixed and placed into a quartz boat and then annealed at 600 °C under Ar atmosphere for 4 h with a heating rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. For the synthesis of SC, 2 g pure PTCDA was annealed at 600 °C under Ar atmosphere for 4 h with a heating rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup>.</div><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The microstructure morphology of P-SC and SC was studied via FE-SEM (Hitachi S-4800) equipped with an X-ray energy-dispersive spectrometer and JEOL JEM-2100F TEM. X-ray diffraction (XRD; Philips, X’Pert Pro) was conducted using Cu Kα radiation (λ = 0.154056 nm) over a 2θ range of 5°–80° to investigate the crystalline structure of P-SC and SC. Raman spectra of P-SC and SC were measured via a Renishaw 2000 system. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis of P-SC and SC was conducted via ESCALAB 250Xi. BET surface area measurements were performed with a Quantachrome instrument (Quabrasorb SI-3MP). Electronic conductivity measurements were carried out at room temperature using the four-point method. The sample disk for the measurement was compacted through a hydraulic system.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Calculations Method</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The first principle was carried out with the Cambridge Sequential Total Energy Package, and in which projected-augmented-wave potential was performed. Moreover, the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof and generalized gradient approximation were carried out in the electronic exchange–correlation energy calculations. In all the calculations, the results were performed to be converged, in which the maximum stress was lower than 0.01 GPa, the maximum force on the atom was lower than 0.01 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup> A<sup>–1</sup>, and the maximum displacement was below 1 × 10<sup>–5</sup> A.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The P-SC or SC/acetylene black/carboxyl methylated cellulose (7:2:1, w/w/w) was dissolved into the solution of C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH/H<sub>2</sub>O (1:2.5, w/w) and stirred for 8 h. The mixture was pasted onto a thin copper foil and then dried at 65 °C in a vacuum oven. The weight of the active material was about 0.8–1.2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The graphite cathode was prepared with a weight ratio of graphite/acetylene black/carboxyl methylated cellulose (8:1:1, w/w/w) and casted onto an aluminum foil. Sodium-ion half cells (SIBs) were assembled with 2032-type coin cell in a glovebox under Ar (H<sub>2</sub>O &lt;0.5 ppm, O<sub>2</sub> &lt;0.5 ppm) using sodium metal as the counter electrode. The sodium-based dual-ion full cells (NDIBs) were assembled with graphite as the cathode; the weight ratio of graphite/P-SC in NDIBs is around 3:1, which is the optimized cathode/anode capacity ratio. The electrolyte used for NDIBs was 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC (6:4, v/v). The separator membrane used for both SIBs and NDIBs was Whatman G/A. The amount of the electrolyte for each cell was around 60 μL for SIBs and 120 μL for NDIBs. The galvanostatic charge/discharge process was investigated via Arbin Instrument BT 2000 model. For SIBs, the charge/discharge voltage range was 3.0–0.01 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves were performed in the electrochemical workstation at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> with a voltage range from 3.0 to 0.01 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). For the NDIBs and the graphite cathode in the SIB, the galvanostatic charge/discharge processes were all investigated with the voltage range of 2.0–4.7 V. All the capacities were calculated from the weight of the anode materials. The anode presodiation process was as the following. The P-SC was assembled for SIBs and cycled for one cycle to allow the formation of SEI in the anode, and then, the P-SC electrode was taken out from the SIBs and assembled for NDIBs for the subsequent measurements.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">It is reported that SC synthesized at a high temperature of 900 °C exhibits high ICE around 80% for SIBs recently, and when the SC was used as an anode for NDIBs, the battery could deliver excellent cycle stability and Coulombic efficiency. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Therefore, in order to acquire high capacity anode with low ICE (&lt;70%) for sodium storage, SC and P-SC were facilely synthesized at a relative low temperature of 600 °C. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) were conducted to investigate the morphology of the as prepared P-SC and SC. The SEM image of P-SC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>) clearly revealed that the structure of P-SC was composed of many microrods, with the diameter of a single microrod about 1 μm. It was clear that the surface of the single microrod was quite smooth, which was confirmed via the SEM and TEM images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b. Furthermore, the transversal striations were observed in both SEM and TEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b,c</a>), which could be interpreted as the oriented crystallite growth of perylene-3,4,9,10-tetracarboxylic dianhydride (PTCDA) during the pyrolysis process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) was performed to investigate some selected micro area of the P-SC. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1d</a>, lattice fringes with a specific orientation were observed, consistent with the oriented crystallite growth of PTCDA, indicating the existence of the localized microcrystalline structure in P-SC. In order to investigate the element distribution of P-SC, energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c) was performed. The EDS elemental mapping showed the uniform distribution of carbon, oxygen, and phosphorus, indicating that the phosphorus atoms had been homogeneously doped into the carbonaceous substrate. The atomic contents of C, O and P were 93.68, 5.22 and 1.09%, respectively.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03648h_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. The morphology of the P-SC. (a) SEM, (b) TEM, (c) EDS elemental mapping of P-SC, and (d) geometric structures for Na adsorption on the C and the P-SC (including P–C, P–O–C and P–O C) monolayer of top views. (e) Density of states (DOS) for the C and the P-SC (including P–C, P–O–C, and P–O C).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03648&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As a comparison, the SEM image of SC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>) revealed that the SC also had a microrod structure with a diameter of a single microrod about 3–5 μm, which was much bigger than that of P-SC. The transversal striations could also be observed in SEM (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>) and TEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c</a>). In addition, unconspicuous lattice fringes were observed in the HRTEM image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2d</a>), indicating the disordered structure of SC. The different morphologies between P-SC and SC (as observed from the SEM and TEM images) were probably because of the phosphorus doping in P-SC. For the nature of the sp<sup>2</sup>-bond of carbon, the electron-rich P-SC lattices could easily lead to “out of plane” bonding configurations, which may induce curvature and closure of the system during growth. Hence, the microrod size of P-SC became smaller. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To further characterize P-SC and SC, XRD and XPS measurements were performed. The XRD patterns (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>) of P-SC and SC exhibit two major humpy peaks at around 24.8° and 43.2°, corresponding to the (002) and (100) planes of SC, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> Moreover, XPS measurements were carried out to further investigate the chemical structures of P-SC and SC. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a> shows the full survey XPS of P-SC. Five peaks at around 18, 135, 192, 285, and 533 eV were assigned to the O 2s, P 2p, P 2s, C 1s, and O 2s, respectively. In addition, the amount of C, O, and P in P-SC calculated via XPS was 93.15, 5.77, and 1.08% by atomic content, respectively, close to the values determined by EDS. The high-resolution P 2p XPS spectrum of P-SC in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3c</a> showed two main peaks at 134.2 and 135.0 eV, corresponding to the bond of P–O and P–C, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> The XPS results indicated that the phosphorus atoms had been successfully and uniformly incorporated into the P-SC. In contrast, the full survey XPS curve of SC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3d</a>) revealed only three peaks at 18, 285, and 533 eV, attributed to O 2s, C 1s, and O 1s, respectively. The amount of C and O in SC was 98.36 and 1.64% by the atomic content, respectively. Besides, Raman spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3e</a>) were used to evaluate the degree of structural formation of P-SC and SC. There are two distinct peaks located at around 1340 and 1485 cm<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to the D band and G band, respectively. Generally, the intensity ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) of D band and G band could be used for gauge for the amount of structure defects. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> It is obvious that the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> increased from 0.971 for SC to 1.098 for P-SC, and this phenomenon might be due to the increased content of phosphorus and oxygen functional groups in P-SC encouraging it to be more disordered. In addition, the increased functional groups in P-SC are beneficial for the electrode to obtain high capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">To evaluate the total specific surface area (SSA) of P-SC and SC, nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms measurements were conducted. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a>, the SSA of P-SC was only 1.15 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, with the pore diameters (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a>) of P-SC around 5–14 nm. As a comparison, the SSA of SC is 9.81 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, with the pore diameters around 20–60 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4c,d</a>). The low surface area of P-SC and SC indicated the close packing of P-SC and SC, which was beneficial for obtaining high volumetric energy density.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to explore the effect of the phosphorus doping, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were implemented with pristine carbon and different phosphorus-doped carbon chemical structures (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d). The bonding between atoms was analyzed from electronic density states. It could be found that the DOS of the SC (named as C) and P-SC with possible structures (including C–P, P–O–C, and P–O C) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e) elucidates that both the SC and P-SC were metallic, which is similar to the reported results. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and 53">(52,53)</a> Besides, remarkable new peaks nearby the Fermi energy level in the conduction band were brought by the incorporation of phosphorus and oxygen atoms into the carbon and forming bonds of C–P, C–P–O–C, or C–P–O. In other words, the phosphorus atom or phosphorus–oxygen bonds incorporated into SC would improve the metallic behavior and interrupt the polarized transport degeneracy of the SC, resulting in a transformation of SC from the intrinsic semiconductor to n-type semiconductor, which is conducive to the fast sodium storage performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 53 and 54">(53,54)</a> We also performed the electronic conductivity of P-SC and SC to meet the DOS result. The electronic conductivity of P-SC was 9.79 × 10<sup>–3</sup> S/cm, which was an order of magnitude larger than that of SC (7.46 × 10<sup>–4</sup> S/cm). Finally, the peaks at the Fermi energy of sodium overlapped with other elements for P-SC with C–P and C–P–O–C bonds (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b,c</a>), indicating interaction between them, while, no peaks at the Fermi energy of sodium for SC and P-SC with C–P–O bonds appeared (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a,d</a>). Furthermore, the Na-adsorption energies of P-SC with C–P were more negative than that of SC, which was consistent with the recent findings. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> Above all, the DFT calculation further indicated that the phosphorus-doping could enhance the sodium storage properties.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity of P-SC and SC, half cells were assembled in a glovebox using sodium metal as the counter electrode. CV curves were performed to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of P-SC and SC. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>, the CV curves of P-SC exhibited a broad peak at 1.0–0.3 V in the initial discharge process, which disappeared in the subsequent cycles. The broad peak was believed to be mainly due to the formation of the SEI. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> Nonetheless, in the subsequent discharge cycles, a sloping region located at 0.25–1.0 V was observed, which was attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup> reacted with the functional groups in the P-SC. In addition, the cathode peak at around 0.01 V was mainly due to the Na<sup>+</sup> insert into the SC. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> During the charge processes, only a humpy peak located at 0.1–1.0 V was observed, which could be interpreted as the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> from the SC and the functional groups. As a comparison, the discharge processes of SC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a>) only revealed one peak at around 0.01 V. This could be due to the lack of P atoms in the SC, leading to less functional group. In addition, this reason could be used to explain the disappearance of the humpy sloping region of 0.5–1.0 V during discharge of SC when compared with P-SC.</div><div class="NLM_p">Different electrochemical properties between P-SC and SC could be further confirmed via the charge/discharge profiles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>). According to the initial discharge/charge curves of P-SC and SC, the discharge/charge capacity of P-SC and SC were 478/283 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 323/180 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to an ICE of 59.2 and 55.7%, respectively. Again, the large irreversible sodium loss during the initial cycle was attributed to the formation of the SEI. Besides, the discharge profiles of P-SC and SC could be divided into two regions. The high voltage region of P-SC and SC at 1.0–0.25 V could be interpreted as the Na<sup>+</sup> reacted with the functional groups in the carbonaceous substrate and the low voltage platform below 0.25 V was due to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the SC. It was clear that the discharge capacity of P-SC (312 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) was higher than that of SC (196 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) at the high voltage region (over 0.25 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), which again could be accounted for more functional groups of P-SC than that of SC. The superior electrochemical property of P-SC could be further demonstrated by rate performance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). Apparently, P-SC showed higher capacity at various current densities than that of SC. The P-SC (vs SC) delivered a capacity of 256 (vs 195), 245 (vs 162), 220 (vs 140), 204 (vs 126), 193 (vs 115), 180 (vs 108), and 131 (vs 41) mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at various current densities of 100, 200, 400, 600, 800, 1000, and 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The corresponding discharge/charge curves of P-SC and SC at various current densities are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a,b</a>, respectively. Notably, when the current density returned back to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 80 cycles at various current densities, the P-SC (vs SC) could still recover a reversible capacity of 252 (vs 171) mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating the excellent rate recyclability of both P-SC and SC.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03648h_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) The rate performance of P-SC and SC at current densities from 100 to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The long cycling of P-SC at a current density of (b) 100 and (c) 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03648&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition to the outstanding rate performance, P-SC also exhibited preeminent long cycle stability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b,c). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b shows the cycle stability of P-SC at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. A reversible capacity of 242 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was obtained even after 500 times of repeated discharge/charge processes (with capacity retention of 89.7% or capacity decay of 0.0206% per cycle, based on the capacity of the second discharge). It was worth mentioning that when the environment temperature decreased to 15 °C, a capacity of 220 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> could still be retained (91.6% capacity maintained with respect to the capacity at 25 °C) at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. When the environment temperature rose back to 25 °C, the capacity still could recover to 240 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. For convenience, the discharge/charge curves of P-SC at different cycles and different temperatures are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>. There was no obvious difference in the charge/discharge platform of P-SC after 500 cycles, indicating the superior cyclability of P-SC. Furthermore, the cycle stability of SC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>) and P-SC (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c) was further investigated at a high current density of 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Notably, a reversible capacity of 89 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> of P-SC still retained even after 3000 cycles, with capacity retention of 68.5% (or capacity decay of 0.010% per cycle) based on the capacity of the second discharge. However, the capacity of SC is relatively low and decayed rapidly. The comparison of the P-SC anode with other carbonaceous anodes reported in previously literatures are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Besides, the rod structure of the P-SC was maintained even after sequential discharge/charge processes. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a–c</a> shows the SEM, TEM, and HRTEM images of the P-SC after 500 cycles at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. It was obvious that the similar rod structure is still retained. As the rod shape morphology could provide the stable framework and electron transportation channels, which was responsible for the superior long cycle stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> On the other hand, the phosphorus doping could significantly reduce the impendence of the SIBs (agrees well with the DFT calculations), which was crucial for the P-SC to achieve superior electrochemical properties (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">In view of the superior rate performance and low ICE (59.2%) of the P-SC, the sodium-based dual-ion full batteries (NDIBs) were assembled to investigate the importance of offset the initial sodium loss in NDIBs. The typical charge/discharge profile of graphite cathode in Na half-cells is presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a</a>. The NDIBs were assembled with the configuration of unsodiated P-SC (anode)|NaPF<sub>6</sub> (electrolyte)|graphite (cathode), named u-NDIBs. It could be seen that the u-NDIBs delivers an initial charge/discharge capacity of 2673/125 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to an ICE of 4.69% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). The extremely low ICE of the u-NDIBs was possibly caused by the large amount of sodium loss in the initial cycle (due to the formation of SEI in the anode) and was detrimental for any practical applications. The rate performance of the u-NDIBs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b) showed discharge capacity of 95, 85, 79, 75, 72, and 69 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 500, 1000, 1500, 2000, 2500, and 3000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In addition, the corresponding charge/discharge profiles of the u-NDIBs at various current densities are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12b</a>. Furthermore, the cycling of the u-NDIBs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c) exhibited a reversible capacity of 79 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles with a capacity retention of 73.1% based on the second discharge. However, the Coulombic efficiency of the u-NDIBs at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> is below 80%. Moreover, when the current density increased to 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d), a poor cycling stability was obtained with a reversible capacity of 44 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles, with a capacity retention of only 47.8% based on the second discharge. The large amount SEI formation in the anode during the initial charge process can be the one utmost reason for the inferior electrochemical properties of the u-NDIBs, further resulting in a poor Coulombic efficiency and inferior cycling stability of the u-NDIBs.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03648h_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of the NDIBs with the configuration of unsodiated P-SC (anode)|1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> (electrolyte)|graphite (cathode), named u-NDIBs. (a) Initial charge/discharge profile of the u-NDIBs at the current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Rate property of the u-NDIBs at various current densities from 500 to 3000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> from the second cycles. (c) Cycling stability of the u-NDIBs at the current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> from the second cycles. (d) Cycling stability of the u-NDIBs at the current density of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03648&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To resolve the above problem, we proposed an anode presodiation method prior to assembling the NDIBs. The detailed anode presodiation process was as the following: the P-SC anode was assembled into SIBs first and was cycled only once to allow the formation of the SEI in the P-SC; then, the P-SC electrode was taken out from the SIBs and assembled the NDIBs for the subsequent measurements, named p-NDIBs. The initial charge/discharge profile of the p-NDIBs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a) exhibited a charge/discharge capacity of 309/112 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to an ICE of 36.2%, which was around 8 times higher than that of u-NDIBs. The typical CV curve at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13a</a>. Moreover, the cycle stability of the NDIBs after presodiation was also improved significantly. It could deliver a reversible capacity over 110 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles without obvious capacity decay (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). The Coulombic efficiency increased to 95% after a few cycles, much higher than that of u-NDIBs (beneath 80%). Furthermore, when a high current density of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> was applied (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), a reversible capacity of 81 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was obtained after 900 cycles with capacity retention of 81.8%, corresponding to a capacity decay of 0.02% per cycle. It was worth mentioning that the Coulombic efficiency was up to 98%, except for the initial several cycles.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03648h_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. The electrochemical performance of the NDIBs with the configuration of presodiated P-SC (anode)|1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> (electrolyte)|graphite (cathode), named p-NDIBs. (a) The initial charge/discharge profile of the p-NDIBs at the current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) The cycling stability of the p-NDIBs at the current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) The cycling stability of the p-NDIBs at the current density of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) The rate property of the p-NDIBs at various current densities from 500 to 3000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03648&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The p-NDIBs not only presented excellent cycle stability and high Coulombic efficiency but also exhibited superior rate performance. The p-NDIBs delivered a discharge capacity of 108, 98, 89, 83, 78, and 73 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 500, 1000, 1500, 2000, 2500, and 3000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). Furthermore, when the p-NDIBs was stored for 24 h after various cycles, it still delivered excellent cycling stability and rate performance, with a reversible capacity of 118 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> even after 200 cycles at various current densities. In particular, the p-NDIBs exhibited Coulombic efficiency over 90% after several cycles, which was much higher than that of u-NDIBs. In addition, when the current density is increased from 1000 to 3000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the Coulombic efficiency is also improved to 95–99.99%. Again, when the current density returned back to 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, a relative high Coulombic efficiency of 95% still could be obtained. The corresponding charge/discharge profiles of the p-NDIBs at various current densities are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13b</a>. It should be noted that the superior electrochemical properties of p-NDIBs are comparable to the previous work (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). Besides, the cycle performance of the NDIBs with the SC anode before and after anode presodiation was also performed. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>, the NDIB after presodiation exhibits more stable cycle performance than that without presodiation, which also further demonstrated the favorable influence of the anode presodiation process on the cycle stability of NDIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the mechanism of the significant improvement in electrochemical performance for NDIBs after presodiation, SEM and XPS measurements of the P-SC electrode of NDIBs and SIBs after the initial cycle were conducted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). The SEM images of the P-SC electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a–c) after the initial cycle revealed the formation of the SEI. In addition, the XPS measurements (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d,e) were conducted to confirm the SEI on the surface of the P-SC electrode. The distinct peaks of F 1s and Na 1s were observed for the both P-SC electrodes in the full survey XPS curves, and the atomic ratio of the F/Na is 20.87%:23.03% and 21.81%:21.31% for the P-SC electrode after presodiation and after the initial cycle of NDIBs, respectively. Moreover, the high-resolution F 1s XPS of the P-SC reflected that the F element for P-SC was from NaF although there was little PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> in the P-SC electrode of NDIBs after the initial cycles. In other words, the formation of NaF during the SEI formation consumed the Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte, which might break the balance of the Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> in the electrolyte.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03648h_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. The SEM image of the P-SC electrode at the state of (a) pristine, (b) after anode presodiation, and (c) after the initial cycle of the NDIBs without anode presodiation. (d) The full XPS survey and (e) high-resolution F 1s XPS of the P-SC electrode. The SEM and XPS have proved the SEI formation during the initial cycles for both NDIBs and SIBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03648&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition, the XRD measurements for graphite cathode were conducted after one cycle for the u-NDIBs and p-NDIBs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a) to investigate the influence of the initial sodium loss which was offset by anode presodiation. It was obvious that the graphite was almost fully recovered for the p-NDIBs, while there was still a humpy peak around 26° for the u-NDIBs. The XRD humpy peak around 26° for the u-NDIBs was probably due to the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> residues in the graphite cathode. This was attributed to the SEI formation during the initial charge process of the u-NDIBs, resulting in large amount of sodium loss and thus the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> residues left in the graphite (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). On the other hand, for the p-NDIBs, the SEI had formed during the presodiation process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c), which could avoid the large amount sodium loss during cycling. Consequently, most of the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> in p-NDIBs could be deserted from the graphite cathode during discharge process, and the graphite would recover more readily (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03648h_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) XRD patterns of the graphite cathode at the state of pristine and after one cycle. (b) Proposed mechanism of the u-NDIBs after one cycle. (c) SEI formation during the presodiation process. (d) Proposed mechanism of the p-NDIBs after one cycle.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/asset/images/large/am-2018-03648h_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03648&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">4.  Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have synthesized a high capacity P-SC anode and demonstrated a valid approach to offset the initial sodium loss via anode presodiation for high performance sodium-based DIBs. The NDIBs with the pre-sodiation process deliver high discharge capacity, excellent rate performance, superior Coulombic efficiency (p-NDIBs vs u-NDIBs: 95 vs 75% after a few cycles at 500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), and long-term cycling stability (p-NDIBs vs u-NDIBs: 81 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 900 cycles with capacity retention of 81.8% vs 44 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention of 47.8%). This work provided a facile method to improve the anode capacity by phosphorus doping and also demonstrated an efficient way to offset the initial sodium loss.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sustainability and in situ monitoring in battery development</span>. <i>Nat.                 10.1038/nmat4777</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D16%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D45-56%26journal%3DNat.%2BMater.%26author%3DC.%2BP.%2BGrey%26author%3DJ.%2BM.%2BTarascon%26title%3DSustainability%2Band%2Bin%2Bsitu%2Bmonitoring%2Bin%2Bbattery%2Bdevelopment%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fnmat4777&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b03648&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fnmat4777&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sustainability and in situ monitoring in battery development</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grey C P; Grey C P; Tarascon J M; Tarascon J M; Tarascon J M</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">45-56</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of improved rechargeable batteries represents a major technological challenge for this new century, as batteries constitute the limiting components in the shift from petrol (gasoline) powered to electric vehicles, while also enabling the use of more renewable energy on the grid.  To minimize the ecological implications associated with their wider use, we must integrate sustainability of battery materials into our research endeavours, choosing chemistries that have a minimum footprint in nature and that are more readily recycled or integrated into a full circular economy.  Sustainability and cost concerns require that we greatly increase the battery lifetime and consider second lives for batteries.  As part of this, we must monitor the state of health of batteries continuously during operation to minimize their degradation.  It is thus important to push the frontiers of operando techniques to monitor increasingly complex processes.  In this Review, we will describe key advances in both more sustainable chemistries and operando techniques, along with some of the remaining challenges and possible solutions, as we personally perceive them.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bommier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span>. <i>Acc. Chem. Res.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>49</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">231</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">240</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D49%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D231-240%26journal%3DAcc.%2BChem.%2BRes.%26author%3DW.%2BLuo%26author%3DF.%2BShen%26author%3DC.%2BBommier%26author%3DH.%2BZhu%26author%3DX.%2BJi%26author%3DL.%2BHu%26title%3DNa-Ion%2BBattery%2BAnodes%253A%2BMaterials%2Band%2BElectrochemistry%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.accounts.5b00482&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b03648&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.accounts.5b00482&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201504589"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Core-Shell Ge@Graphene@TiO<sub>2</sub> Nanofibers as a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Lithium and Sodium Ion Battery</span>. <i>Adv. Funct. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>26</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1104</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1111</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adfm.201504589</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D26%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D1104-1111%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26author%3DX.%2BWang%26author%3DL.%2BFan%26author%3DD.%2BGong%26author%3DJ.%2BZhu%26author%3DQ.%2BZhang%26author%3DB.%2BLu%26title%3DCore-Shell%2BGe%2540Graphene%2540TiO2%2BNanofibers%2Bas%2Ba%2BHigh-Capacity%2Band%2BCycle-Stable%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BLithium%2Band%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBattery%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.201504589&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b03648&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.201504589&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Core-Shell Ge@graphene@TiO2 Nanofibers as a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Lithium and Sodium Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Xiaoyan; Fan, Ling; Gong, Decai; Zhu, Jian; Zhang, Qingfeng; Lu, Bingan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1104-1111</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ge is considered as a promising anode material because of its comparable Li and Na storage capability, but it usually exhibits poor cycling stability due to the large vol. variation during Li or Na uptake and release processes.  Ge@graphene nanofibers are 1st obtained through electrospinning followed by calcination.  Then at. layer deposition is used to fabricate Ge@graphene@TiO2 core-shell nanofibers (Ge@G@TiO2 NFs) as anode materials for Li and Na ion batteries (LIBs and SIBs).  Graphene and TiO2 can double protect the Ge nanofibers in charge and discharge processes.  The Ge@G@TiO2 NFs composite as an anode material is versatile and exhibits enhanced electrochem. performance for LIBs and SIBs.  The capacity of the Ge@G@TiO2 NFs composite can be maintained at 1050 mA h g-1 (100th cycle) and 182 mA h g-1 (250th cycle) for LIBs and SIBs, resp., at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1, showing high capacity and good cycling stability (much better than that of Ge nanofibers or Ge@G nanofibers).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201402943"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ultrasmall Sn Nanoparticles Embedded in Carbon as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The Unique Structural Evolution of the O3-Phase Na2/3Fe2/3Mn1/3O2 during High Rate Charge/Discharge: A Sodium-Centred Perspective</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sharma, Neeraj; Gonzalo, Elena; Pramudita, James C.; Han, Man Huon; Brand, Helen E.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of new insertion electrodes in sodium-ion batteries requires an in-depth understanding of the relationship between electrochem. performance and the structural evolution during cycling.  To date in situ synchrotron X-ray and neutron diffraction methods appear to be the only probes of in situ electrode evolution at high rates, a crit. condition for battery development.  Here, the structural evolution of the recently synthesized O3-phase of Na2/3Fe2/3Mn1/3O2 is reported under relatively high current rates.  The evolution of the phases, their lattice parameters, and phase fractions, and the sodium content in the crystal structure as a function of the charge/discharge process are shown.  It is found that the O3-phase persists throughout the charge/discharge cycle but undergoes a series of two-phase and solid-soln. transitions subtly modifying the sodium content and at. positions but keeping the overall space-group symmetry (structural motif).  In addn., for the first time, evidence of a structurally characterized region is shown that undergoes two-phase and solid-soln. phase transitions simultaneously.  The Mn/Fe-O bond lengths, c lattice parameter evolution, and the distance between the Mn/FeO6 layers are shown to concertedly change in a favorable manner for Na+ insertion/extn.  The exceptional electrochem. performance of this electrode can be related in part to the electrode maintaining the O3-phase throughout the charge/discharge process.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1039/c6ee01750a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Clément, R.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structurally stable Mg-doped P2-Na2/3Mn1-yMgyO2 sodium-ion battery cathodes with high rate performance: insights from electrochemical, NMR and diffraction studies</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Clement, Raphaele J.; Billaud, Juliette; Robert Armstrong, A.; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo; Bruce, Peter G.; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are a more sustainable alternative to the existing lithium-ion technol. and could alleviate some of the stress on the global lithium market as a result of the growing elec. car and portable electronics industries.  Fundamental research focused on understanding the structural and electronic processes occurring on electrochem. cycling is key to devising rechargeable batteries with improved performance.  We present an in-depth investigation of the effect of Mg doping on the electrochem. performance and structural stability of Na2/3MnO2 with a P2 layer stacking by comparing three compns.: Na2/3Mn1-yMgyO2 (y = 0.0, 0.05, 0.1).  We show that Mg substitution leads to smoother electrochem., with fewer distinct electrochem. processes, improved rate performance and better capacity retention.  These observations are attributed to the more gradual structural changes upon charge and discharge, as obsd. with synchrotron, powder X-ray, and neutron diffraction.  Mg doping reduces the no. of Mn3+ Jahn-Teller centers and delays the high voltage phase transition occurring in P2-Na2/3MnO2.  The local structure is investigated using 23Na solid-state NMR (ssNMR) spectroscopy.  The ssNMR data provide direct evidence for fewer oxygen layer shearing events, leading to a stabilized P2 phase, and an enhanced Na-ion mobility up to 3.8 V vs.  Na+/Na upon Mg doping.  The 5% Mg-doped phase exhibits one of the best rate performances reported to date for sodium-ion cathodes with a P2 structure, with a reversible capacity of 106 mA h g-1 at the very high discharge rate of 5000 mA g-1.  In addn., its structure is highly reversible and stable cycling is obtained between 1.5 and 4.0 V vs.  Na+/Na, with a capacity of approx. 140 mA h g-1 retained after 50 cycles at a rate of 1000 mA g-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1021/acscentsci.5b00400"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Licht, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Douglas, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carter, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lefler, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pint, C.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The cost and practicality of greenhouse gas removal processes, which are crit. for environmental sustainability, pivot on high-value secondary applications derived from carbon capture and conversion techniques.  Using the solar thermal electrochem. process (STEP), ambient CO2 captured in molten lithiated carbonates leads to the prodn. of carbon nanofibers (CNFs) and carbon nanotubes (CNTs) at high yield through electrolysis using inexpensive steel electrodes.  These low-cost CO2-derived CNTs and CNFs are demonstrated as high performance energy storage materials in both lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries.  Owing to synthetic control of sp3 content in the synthesized nanostructures, optimized storage capacities are measured over 370 mAh g-1 (lithium) and 130 mAh g-1 (sodium) with no capacity fade under durability tests up to 200 and 600 cycles, resp.  This work demonstrates that ambient CO2, considered as an environmental pollutant, can be attributed economic value in grid-scale and portable energy storage systems with STEP scale-up practicality in the context of combined cycle natural gas elec. power generation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/c6ee01501h"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Antimony (Sb) is an attractive anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with a high theor. capacity of 660 mAh g-1.  However, its practical application is greatly hindered by the rapid capacity fading which is largely due to the large vol. expansion during sodiation.  Tuning the morphol. and structure at the nanoscale or using carbonaceous materials as the buffer layer is essential to address this issue.  Here, a facile carbon-coating coupled with a thermal-redn. strategy has been developed to synthesize unique Sb@C coaxial nanotubes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hexagonal Na3Zr2PO4(SiO4)2 is a well-known fast Na+ conductor commonly referred to as NASICON.  The low cost and wide availability of sodium invites exploration of Na+-insertion cathodes with the hexagonal NASICON framework structure.  Here we report a low-temp. synthesis of NaFe2PO4(SO4)2 with the same host framework as NASICON and its performance as the cathode of a Na/Na1+xFe2PO4(SO4)2 cell operating on the Fe3+/Fe2+ redox couple.  The cell provides a single-phase reaction having a capacity approaching 100 mA h g-1 at 0.1C after 50 cycles over the voltage range 2 ≤ V ≤ 4 V with a coulomb efficiency approaching 100%.  An increase in capacity with cycling is the result of aging of an unoptimized electrode morphol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201601196"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chu, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Potassium-based dual-ion full batteries (PDIBs) were developed with graphite anode, polytriphenylamine (PTPAn) cathode, and KPF6-based electrolyte.  The PDIBs delivered a reversible capacity of 60 mA-h g-1 at a median discharge voltage of 3.23 V at 50 mA g-1, with superior rate performance and long-term cycling stability over 500 cycles (capacity retention of 75.5%).  Unlike the traditional dual-ion batteries, the operation mechanism of the PDIBs with PTPAn cathode is that the PF6- ions interacted with the nitrogen atom reversibly in the PTPAn cathode and the K+ ions were intercalated/deintercalated into/from the graphite anode during the charge/discharge process.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201800804"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Nonaqueous Potassium-Based Battery-Supercapacitor Hybrid Device</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Covalent sulfur for advanced room temperature sodium-sulfur batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Ling; Ma, Ruifang; Yang, Yuhua; Chen, Suhua; Lu, Bingan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The room temp. sodium-sulfur (RT Na-S) batteries have attracted extensive attention due to its low cost and high specific energy.  Yet, RT Na-S batteries usually suffer from low reversible capacity, short lifespan and inferior Coulombic efficiency.  Herein, covalent sulfur based carbonaceous materials was investigated for RT Na-S battery to address these drawbacks.  Generally, the covalent S could prevent the formation of sodium polysulfide (Na2Sn, 4≤n≤8) effectively, further avoid the dissoln. of sodium polysulfide into electrolyte; besides, the covalent S in these carbonaceous materials is reversible during charge/discharge process, which is good for cycling stability.  Benefiting from these merits, the covalent sulfur based materials delivers high reversible capacity over 1000 mA h g-1, long cycling stability for 900 cycles with capacity decay of 0.053% per cycle and superior Coulombic efficiency approx. 100%.  This study may provide a new method for designing high performance RT Na-S batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201403257"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ambient temperature sodium-sulfur batteries</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2108</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2114</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ambient- or room-temp. sodium-sulfur batteries are gaining much attention as a low-cost option for large-scale elec. energy storage applications.  However, their adoption is hampered by severe challenges.  This concept paper summarizes first the operating principles, history, recent progress, and challenges of room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery technol., and then suggests future directions towards enhancing performance in order for it to be a viable technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201502185"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kohl, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Borrmann, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Althues, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kaskel, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard Carbon Anodes and Novel Electrolytes for Long-Cycle-Life Room Temperature Sodium-Sulfur Full Cell Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.
