                        cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A stable room-temperature sodium-sulfur battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Shuya; Xu, Shaomao; Agrawral, Akanksha; Choudhury, Snehashis; Lu, Yingying; Tu, Zhengyuan; Ma, Lin; Archer, Lynden A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11722</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-energy rechargeable batteries based on earth-abundant materials are important for mobile and stationary storage technologies.  Rechargeable sodium-sulfur batteries able to operate stably at room temp. are among the most sought-after platforms because such cells take advantage of a two-electron-redox process to achieve high storage capacity from inexpensive electrode materials.  Here we report a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery that uses a microporous carbon-sulfur composite cathode, and a liq. carbonate electrolyte contg. the ionic liq. 1-methyl-3-propylimidazolium-chlorate tethered to SiO2 nanoparticles.  We show that these cells can cycle stably at a rate of 0.5 C (1 C=1675, mAh g-1) with 600 mAh g-1 reversible capacity and nearly 100% Coulombic efficiency.  By means of spectroscopic and electrochem. anal., we find that the particles form a sodium-ion conductive film on the anode, which stabilizes deposition of sodium.  We also find that sulfur remains interred in the carbon pores and undergo solid-state electrochem. reactions with sodium ions.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393288"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seel, J.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical intercalation of PF6 into graphite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Seel, J. A.; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nonaq. electrochem. cells using carbon as both the neg. and pos. electrodes have been proposed in the literature.  In such "dual graphite" cells, lithium intercalates into the neg. electrode, and the anion intercalates into the pos. electrode when the cells are charged, depleting the electrolyte of salt.  Here, the origin of the cell potential is considered first.  Then, using Li/graphite test cells, PF6 intercalation into graphite is studied using electrochem. methods and in situ X-ray diffraction.  We prove that PF6 intercalated graphite exists in a series of staged phases.  Differential capacity vs. voltage measurements are used to det. the voltages of the staging transitions and the compns. of the staged LiPF6 phases.  The cell potential during anion intercalation rises to over 5 V, and we have learned that cells with electrolytes using Et Me sulfone solvent can give reliable results up to 5.5 V under these highly oxidizing conditions.  By contrast, ethylene carbonate based electrolytes are strongly oxidized above about 5.2 V, preventing complete loading of graphite with PF6.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee43333a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Read, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, dual-ion cells based on the anion intercalation into a graphite pos. electrode have been proposed as electrochem. energy storage devices.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A redox-active metal-org. framework, Fe2(dobpdc) (dobpdc4- = 4,4'-dioxidobiphenyl-3,3'-dicarboxylate), is shown to undergo a topotactic oxidative insertion reaction with a variety of weakly coordinating anions, including BF4- and PF6-.  The reaction results in just a minor lattice contraction, and a broad intervalence charge-transfer band emerges, indicative of charge mobility.  Although both metal- and ligand-based oxidns. can be accessed, only the former were found to be fully reversible and, importantly, proceed stoichiometrically under both chem. and electrochem. conditions.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anion Hosting Cathodes in Dual-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Rodriguez-Perez, Ismael A.; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1762-1770</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AELCCP</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2380-8195</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Dual-ion batteries operate on two intercalants: anions for the cathode and cations for the anode.  This battery was initially known as a dual-graphite battery, where both electrodes are graphitic carbon.  The primary challenge of dual-graphite batteries is the very high operation potential of the cathode, often requiring an upper cutoff potential above 5 V vs Li+/Li.  Such a potential readily oxidizes alkyl and alkylene carbonate-based electrolytes.  The anode side, in fact, can employ any anode of most metal-ion batteries, although, to date, the focus has still been the Li-graphite anode.  Recent progress has significantly advanced the technol. readiness level for this battery.  Additives or ionic liq. electrolytes help mitigate cathode irreversibility; nongraphite anodes, such as aluminum, allow new carbonate electrolytes that lack the necessity of ethylene carbonate; nongraphite cathodes, including metal-org. frameworks and polycyclic arom. hydrocarbons have exhibited a remarkable potential.  This Perspective highlights the challenges, summarizes the recent progress, and attempts to point out future directions in the field.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201703951"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Penne-Like MoS<sub>2</sub>/Carbon Nanocomposite as Anode for Sodium-Ion-Based Dual-Ion Battery</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>14</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1703951</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">26</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7445-7449</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. Na-ion batteries (SIBs) showed great promise in grid-scale energy storage, portable electronics, and elec. vehicles because of the abundance of low-cost Na.  Na-based layered oxides with a P2-type layered framework have been considered as one of the most promising cathode materials for SIBs.  However, they suffer from the undesired P2-O2 phase transition, which leads to rapid capacity decay and limited reversible capacities.  Herein, this problem can be significantly mitigated by substituting some of the Ni ions with Mg to obtain Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33-xMgxO2 (0≤x≤0.33).  Both the reversible capacity and the capacity retention of the P2-type cathode material were remarkably improved as the P2-O2 phase transition was thus suppressed during cycling.  This strategy might also be applicable to the modulation of the phys. and chem. properties of layered oxides and provides new insight into the rational design of high-capacity and highly stable cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee44004d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-quality Prussian blue crystals as superior cathode materials for room-temperature sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. intercalation of PF-6 into various carbons was studied for the cathode of an Li-ion rechargeable battery.  The capacity for electrochem. intercalation of PF-6 increases with decreasing (002) plane distance, i.e. increasing the crystallinity of graphitic-C - the largest capacity was achieved with C with a nearly ideal graphitic-C structure.  By using graphitic C with high crystallinity and a stable electrolyte of ethylene carbonate-dimethylene carbonate, a rechargeable battery with high capacity (90 mA-h/g) and rate capability was demonstrated.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref39" class="refNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit39" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.6b00300"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rodríguez-Pérez, I.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors demonstrated, for the 1st time, a polycyclic arom. hydrocarbon (PAH), cryst. and readily available coronene, exhibits highly reversible anion-storage properties.  Conventional graphite anion-insertion electrodes operate at potentials &gt;4.5 V vs. Li+/Li, requiring electrolyte additives or the use of ionic liqs. as electrolytes.  The coronene electrode shows flat plateaus at 4.2 V (charge) and 4.0 V (discharge) in a std. alkyl carbonate electrolyte and delivers a reversible discharge capacity of ∼40 mA-h/g.  Ex situ characterization reveals that coronene retains its cryst. structure and chem. bonding upon initial PF6- incorporation.  Coronene-PF6 electrodes show impressive cycling stability: 92% capacity retention after 960 cycles.  The discovery of the reversible anion-storage properties of coronene may open new avenues toward dual-ion batteries based on PAHs as electrodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref40" class="refNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit40" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201603735"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon-Coated Porous Aluminum Foil Anode for High-Rate, Long-Term Cycling Stability, and High Energy Density Dual-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Capacity Te Anode Confined in Microporous Carbon for Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Juan; Yin, Ya-Xia; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">50</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable attention as an alternative energy-storage technol. in recent years.  Developing advanced sodium storage anode materials with appropriate working potential, high capacity, and good cycling performance is very important.  Herein, we demonstrate a nanostructured tellurium@carbon (nano-Te@C) composite by confining nano-Te mols. in the space of carbon micropores as an attractive anode material for SIBs.  The nano-Te@C anode presents an appropriate redox potential in the range of 1.05-1.35 V (vs Na+/Na), which avoids the Na dendrite problem and achieves a high reversible capacity of 410 mA h g-1 on the basis of a two-electron redox reaction mechanism.  Notably, the nano-Te@C exhibits an admirable long-term cycling stability with a high capacity retention of 90% for 1000 cycles (i.e., ultralow capacity decay of 0.01% per cycle).  The excellent electrochem. property of nano-Te@C benefits from the high electroactivity from the nanostructure design and the effective confinement of the microporous carbon host.  In addn., a Na-ion full cell by using nano-Te@C as anode and Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 as cathode is demonstrated for the first time and exhibits a remarkable capacity retention up to 95% after 150 cycles.  The results put new insights for the development of advanced SIBs with long-cycle lifespan.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref44" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1039/c6ta07589d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">An O3-type NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cathode for sodium-ion batteries with improved rate performance and cycling stability</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. behavior of Na-ion and Li-ion full cells was investigated, using hard carbon as the anode material, and NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 and LiNi0.5Mn0.5O2 as the cathodes.  A detailed description of the structure, phase transition, electrochem. behavior and kinetics of the NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cathodes is presented, including interesting comparison with their lithium analog.  The crit. effect of the hard carbon anodes pretreatment in the total capacity and stability of full cells is clearly demonstrated.  Using impedance spectroscopy in three electrodes cells, we show that the full cell impedance is dominated by the contribution of the cathode side.  We discuss possible reasons for capacity fading of these systems, its connection to the cathode structure and relevant surface phenomena.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref48" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1021/cm502481b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oh, S.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Scrosati, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amine, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Capacity O<sub>3</sub>-Type Na[Li<sub>0.05</sub>(Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>0.95</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Cathode for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High Capacity O3-Type Na[Li0.05(Ni0.25Fe0.25Mn0.5)0.95]O2 Cathode for Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Oh, Seung-Min; Myung, Seung-Taek; Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Scrosati, Bruno; Amine, Khalil; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report Na[Li0.05(Ni0.25Fe0.25Mn0.5)0.95]O2 layered cathode materials were synthesized via a copptn. method.  The Na[Li0.05(Ni0.25Fe0.25Mn0.5)0.95]O2 electrode exhibited an exceptionally high capacity (180.1 mA h g-1 at 0.1 C-rate) as well as excellent capacity retentions (0.2 C-rate: 89.6%, 0.5 C-rate: 92.1%) and rate capabilities at various C-rates (0.1 C-rate: 180.1 mA h g-1, 1 C-rate: 130.9 mA h g-1, 5 C-rate: 96.2 mA h g-1), which were achieved due to the Li supporting structural stabilization by introduction into the transition metal layer.                         cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemically Expandable Soft Carbon as Anodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Jian, Zelang; Xing, Zhenyu; Wang, Wei; Bommier, Clement; Lerner, Michael M.; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">516-522</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (NIBs) have attracted great attention for scalable elec. energy storage considering the abundance and wide availability of Na resources.  However, it remains elusive whether carbon anodes can achieve the similar scale of successes in Na-ion batteries as in Li-ion batteries.  Currently, much attention is focused on hard carbon while soft carbon is generally considered a poor choice.  In this study, we discover that soft carbon can be a high-rate anode in NIBs if the prepn. conditions are carefully chosen.  Furthermore, we discover that the turbostratic lattice of soft carbon is electrochem. expandable, where d-spacing rises from 3.6 to 4.2 Å.  Such a scale of lattice expansion only due to the Na-ion insertion was not known for carbon materials.  It is further learned that portions of such lattice expansion are highly reversible, resulting in excellent cycling performance.  Moreover, soft carbon delivers a good capacity at potentials above 0.2 V, which enables an intrinsically dendrite-free anode for NIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref51" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1016/0022-0248(92)90658-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Möbus, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Karl, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kobayashi, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structure of perylene-tetracarboxylic-dianhydride thin films on alkali halide crystal substrates</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Phosphorus and Nitrogen Dual-Doped Few-Layered Porous Graphene: A High-Performance Anode Material for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ma, Xinlong; Ning, Guoqing; Qi, Chuanlei; Xu, Chenggen; Gao, Jinsen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Few-layered graphene networks composed of phosphorus and nitrogen dual-doped porous graphene (PNG) are synthesized via a MgO-templated chem. vapor deposition (CVD) using (NH4)3PO4 as N and P source.  P and N atoms have been substitutionally doped in graphene networks since the doping takes place at the same time with the graphene growth in the CVD process.  Raman spectra show that the amt. of defects or disorders increases after P and N atoms are incorporated into graphene frameworks.  The doping levels of P and N measured by XPS are 0.6 and 2.6 at %, resp.  As anodes for Li ion batteries (LIBs), the PNG electrode exhibits high reversible capacity (2250 mAh/g at the c.d. of 50 mA/g), excellent rate capability (750 mAh/g at 1000 mA/g), and satisfactory cycling stability (no capacity decay after 1500 cycles), showing much enhanced electrode performance as compared to the undoped few-layered porous graphene.  Our results show that the PNG is a promising candidate for anode materials in high-rate LIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref54" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201301870"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mahmood, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of phosphorus-doped graphene and its multifunctional applications for oxygen reduction reaction and lithium ion batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Synthesis of phosphorus-doped graphene and its multifunctional applications for oxygen redn. reaction and lithium ion batteries are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref55" class="refNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit55" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702781"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amine, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insights into the Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Mechanism of Phosphorus-Functionalized Hard Carbon as Ultrahigh Capacity Anodes</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1702781</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201702781</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D8%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D1702781%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DY.%2BLi%26author%3DY.%2BYuan%26author%3DY.%2BBai%26author%3DY.%2BLiu%26author%3DZ.%2BWang%26author%3DL.%2BLi%26author%3DF.%2BWu%26author%3DK.%2BAmine%26author%3DC.%2BWu%26author%3DJ.%2BLu%26title%3DInsights%2Binto%2Bthe%2BNa%252B%2BStorage%2BMechanism%2Bof%2BPhosphorus-Functionalized%2BHard%2BCarbon%2Bas%2BUltrahigh%2BCapacity%2BAnodes%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.201702781&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b03648&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.201702781&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref56" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201600565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reactive Oxygen-Doped 3D Interdigital Carbonaceous Materials for Li and Na Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2783</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2791</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/smll.201600565</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D12%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D2783-2791%26journal%3DSmall%26author%3DL.%2BFan%26author%3DB.%2BLu%26title%3DReactive%2BOxygen-Doped%2B3D%2BInterdigital%2BCarbonaceous%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BLi%2Band%2BNa%2BIon%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fsmll.201600565&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b03648&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600565&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reactive Oxygen-Doped 3D Interdigital Carbonaceous Materials for Li- and Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Ling; Lu, Bingan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2783-2791</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMALBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1613-6810</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbonaceous materials as anodes usually exhibit low capacity for Li ion batteries (LIBs) and Na ion batteries (SIBs).  O-doped carbonaceous materials have the potential of high capacity and super rate performance.  However the reported O-doped carbonaceous materials usually exhibit inferior electrochem. performance.  To overcome this problem, a high reactive O-doped 3-dimensional interdigital porous carbonaceous material is designed and synthesized through epitaxial growth method and used as anodes for LIBs and SIBs.  It delivers high reversible capacity, super rate performance, and long cycling stability (473 mA h g-1 after 500 cycles for LIBs and 223 mA h g-1 after 1200 cycles for SIBs, resp., at the c.d. of 1000 mA g-1), with a capacity decay of 0.0214% per cycle for LIBs and 0.0155% per cycle for SIBs.  Constructing 3-dimensional interdigital porous structure with reactive O functional groups can significantly enhance the electrochem. performance of O-doped carbonaceous material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="931888aaea3654b8-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48'],'ref49':['cit49'],'ref50':['cit50'],'ref51':['cit51'],'ref52':['cit52'],'ref53':['cit53'],'ref54':['cit54'],'ref55':['cit55'],'ref56':['cit56']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

    font-weight: 500;

        </p><ol id="rightTab-references" class="useLabel"><li id="rightTab-ref1"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref1" class="rightTabRefNumLink">1</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit1" data-doi="10.1038/nmat4777"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grey, C. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J. M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sustainability and in situ monitoring in battery development</span>. <i>Nat. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>16</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">45</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">56</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/nmat4777</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sustainability and in situ monitoring in battery development</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grey C P; Grey C P; Tarascon J M; Tarascon J M; Tarascon J M</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">45-56</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of improved rechargeable batteries represents a major technological challenge for this new century, as batteries constitute the limiting components in the shift from petrol (gasoline) powered to electric vehicles, while also enabling the use of more renewable energy on the grid.  To minimize the ecological implications associated with their wider use, we must integrate sustainability of battery materials into our research endeavours, choosing chemistries that have a minimum footprint in nature and that are more readily recycled or integrated into a full circular economy.  Sustainability and cost concerns require that we greatly increase the battery lifetime and consider second lives for batteries.  As part of this, we must monitor the state of health of batteries continuously during operation to minimize their degradation.  It is thus important to push the frontiers of operando techniques to monitor increasingly complex processes.  In this Review, we will describe key advances in both more sustainable chemistries and operando techniques, along with some of the remaining challenges and possible solutions, as we personally perceive them.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bommier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span>. <i>Acc. Chem. Res.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>49</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">231</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">240</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201504589"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Core-Shell Ge@Graphene@TiO<sub>2</sub> Nanofibers as a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Lithium and Sodium Ion Battery</span>. <i>Adv. Funct.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Ge is considered as a promising anode material because of its comparable Li and Na storage capability, but it usually exhibits poor cycling stability due to the large vol. variation during Li or Na uptake and release processes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of new insertion electrodes in sodium-ion batteries requires an in-depth understanding of the relationship between electrochem. performance and the structural evolution during cycling.  To date in situ synchrotron X-ray and neutron diffraction methods appear to be the only probes of in situ electrode evolution at high rates, a crit. condition for battery development.  Here, the structural evolution of the recently synthesized O3-phase of Na2/3Fe2/3Mn1/3O2 is reported under relatively high current rates.  The evolution of the phases, their lattice parameters, and phase fractions, and the sodium content in the crystal structure as a function of the charge/discharge process are shown.  It is found that the O3-phase persists throughout the charge/discharge cycle but undergoes a series of two-phase and solid-soln. transitions subtly modifying the sodium content and at. positions but keeping the overall space-group symmetry (structural motif).  In addn., for the first time, evidence of a structurally characterized region is shown that undergoes two-phase and solid-soln. phase transitions simultaneously.  The Mn/Fe-O bond lengths, c lattice parameter evolution, and the distance between the Mn/FeO6 layers are shown to concertedly change in a favorable manner for Na+ insertion/extn.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are a more sustainable alternative to the existing lithium-ion technol. and could alleviate some of the stress on the global lithium market as a result of the growing elec. car and portable electronics industries.  Fundamental research focused on understanding the structural and electronic processes occurring on electrochem. cycling is key to devising rechargeable batteries with improved performance.  We present an in-depth investigation of the effect of Mg doping on the electrochem. performance and structural stability of Na2/3MnO2 with a P2 layer stacking by comparing three compns.: Na2/3Mn1-yMgyO2 (y = 0.0, 0.05, 0.1).  We show that Mg substitution leads to smoother electrochem., with fewer distinct electrochem. processes, improved rate performance and better capacity retention.  These observations are attributed to the more gradual structural changes upon charge and discharge, as obsd. with synchrotron, powder X-ray, and neutron diffraction.  Mg doping reduces the no. of Mn3+ Jahn-Teller centers and delays the high voltage phase transition occurring in P2-Na2/3MnO2.  The local structure is investigated using 23Na solid-state NMR (ssNMR) spectroscopy.  The ssNMR data provide direct evidence for fewer oxygen layer shearing events, leading to a stabilized P2 phase, and an enhanced Na-ion mobility up to 3.8 V vs.  Na+/Na upon Mg doping.  The 5% Mg-doped phase exhibits one of the best rate performances reported to date for sodium-ion cathodes with a P2 structure, with a reversible capacity of 106 mA h g-1 at the very high discharge rate of 5000 mA g-1.  In addn., its structure is highly reversible and stable cycling is obtained between 1.5 and 4.0 V vs.  Na+/Na, with a capacity of approx. 140 mA h g-1 retained after 50 cycles at a rate of 1000 mA g-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1021/acscentsci.5b00400"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Licht, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Douglas, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carter, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lefler, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pint, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The cost and practicality of greenhouse gas removal processes, which are crit. for environmental sustainability, pivot on high-value secondary applications derived from carbon capture and conversion techniques.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Antimony (Sb) is an attractive anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with a high theor. capacity of 660 mAh g-1.  However, its practical application is greatly hindered by the rapid capacity fading which is largely due to the large vol. expansion during sodiation.  Tuning the morphol. and structure at the nanoscale or using carbonaceous materials as the buffer layer is essential to address this issue.  Here, a facile carbon-coating coupled with a thermal-redn. strategy has been developed to synthesize unique Sb@C coaxial nanotubes.  With different annealing time, the hollow space and the amt. of Sb inside the tube can be easily tuned by the partial evapn. of Sb.  The as-obtained Sb@C nanotubes exhibit excellent sodium storage properties.  The remarkable electrochem. performance results from the unique coaxial nanoarchitecture.  Specifically, it delivers a high specific capacity of 407 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1 after 240 cycles.  Furthermore, a stable capacity of 240 mAh g-1 can be retained at 1.0 A g-1 even after 2000 cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The room temp. sodium-sulfur (RT Na-S) batteries have attracted extensive attention due to its low cost and high specific energy.  Yet, RT Na-S batteries usually suffer from low reversible capacity, short lifespan and inferior Coulombic efficiency.  Herein, covalent sulfur based carbonaceous materials was investigated for RT Na-S battery to address these drawbacks.  Generally, the covalent S could prevent the formation of sodium polysulfide (Na2Sn, 4≤n≤8) effectively, further avoid the dissoln. of sodium polysulfide into electrolyte; besides, the covalent S in these carbonaceous materials is reversible during charge/discharge process, which is good for cycling stability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">High-energy rechargeable batteries based on earth-abundant materials are important for mobile and stationary storage technologies.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Nonaq. electrochem. cells using carbon as both the neg. and pos. electrodes have been proposed in the literature. Here, the origin of the cell potential is considered first.  Then, using Li/graphite test cells, PF6 intercalation into graphite is studied using electrochem. methods and in situ X-ray diffraction. Pyr14TFSI-LiTFSI is used in combination with the SEI-forming additive ethylene sulfite (ES) which allows stable and highly reversible Li+ ion and TFSI- anion intercalation/de-intercalation into/from the graphite anode and cathode, resp.  By addn. of ES, also the discharge capacity for the anion intercalation can be remarkably increased from 50 mA h g-1 to 97 mA h g-1.  X-ray diffraction studies of the anion intercalation into graphite are conducted in order to understand the influence of the electrolyte additive on the graphite structure and on the cell performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref22" class="rightTabRefNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit22" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.5b08022"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aubrey, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Dual-ion batteries operate on two intercalants: anions for the cathode and cations for the anode.  This battery was initially known as a dual-graphite battery, where both electrodes are graphitic carbon.  The primary challenge of dual-graphite batteries is the very high operation potential of the cathode, often requiring an upper cutoff potential above 5 V vs Li+/Li.  Such a potential readily oxidizes alkyl and alkylene carbonate-based electrolytes.  The anode side, in fact, can employ any anode of most metal-ion batteries, although, to date, the focus has still been the Li-graphite anode.  Recent progress has significantly advanced the technol. readiness level for this battery.  Additives or ionic liq. electrolytes help mitigate cathode irreversibility; nongraphite anodes, such as aluminum, allow new carbonate electrolytes that lack the necessity of ethylene carbonate; nongraphite cathodes, including metal-org. frameworks and polycyclic arom. hydrocarbons have exhibited a remarkable potential.  This Perspective highlights the challenges, summarizes the recent progress, and attempts to point out future directions in the field.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref32" class="rightTabRefNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit32" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201703951"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Penne-Like MoS<sub>2</sub>/Carbon Nanocomposite as Anode for Sodium-Ion-Based Dual-Ion Battery</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>14</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1703951</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Suppressing the P2-O2 Phase Transition of Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33O2 by Magnesium Substitution for Improved Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Peng-Fei; You, Ya; Yin, Ya-Xia; Wang, Yue-Sheng; Wan, Li-Jun; Gu, Lin; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7445-7449</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. Na-ion batteries (SIBs) showed great promise in grid-scale energy storage, portable electronics, and elec. vehicles because of the abundance of low-cost Na.  Na-based layered oxides with a P2-type layered framework have been considered as one of the most promising cathode materials for SIBs.  However, they suffer from the undesired P2-O2 phase transition, which leads to rapid capacity decay and limited reversible capacities.  Herein, this problem can be significantly mitigated by substituting some of the Ni ions with Mg to obtain Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33-xMgxO2 (0≤x≤0.33).  Both the reversible capacity and the capacity retention of the P2-type cathode material were remarkably improved as the P2-O2 phase transition was thus suppressed during cycling.  This strategy might also be applicable to the modulation of the phys. and chem. properties of layered oxides and provides new insight into the rational design of high-capacity and highly stable cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee44004d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-quality Prussian blue crystals as superior cathode materials for room-temperature sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable attention as an alternative energy-storage technol. in recent years.  Developing advanced sodium storage anode materials with appropriate working potential, high capacity, and good cycling performance is very important.  Herein, we demonstrate a nanostructured tellurium@carbon (nano-Te@C) composite by confining nano-Te mols. in the space of carbon micropores as an attractive anode material for SIBs.  The nano-Te@C anode presents an appropriate redox potential in the range of 1.05-1.35 V (vs Na+/Na), which avoids the Na dendrite problem and achieves a high reversible capacity of 410 mA h g-1 on the basis of a two-electron redox reaction mechanism.  Notably, the nano-Te@C exhibits an admirable long-term cycling stability with a high capacity retention of 90% for 1000 cycles (i.e., ultralow capacity decay of 0.01% per cycle).  The excellent electrochem. property of nano-Te@C benefits from the high electroactivity from the nanostructure design and the effective confinement of the microporous carbon host.  In addn., a Na-ion full cell by using nano-Te@C as anode and Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 as cathode is demonstrated for the first time and exhibits a remarkable capacity retention up to 95% after 150 cycles.  The results put new insights for the development of advanced SIBs with long-cycle lifespan.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1039/c6ta07589d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">An O3-type NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cathode for sodium-ion batteries with improved rate performance and cycling stability</span>. <i>J. A Na//NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cell can deliver a reversible capacity of 141 mA h g-1 in the voltage range of 2.0-4.0 V and show a good capacity retention of 90% after 100 cycles.  A highly reversible structural evolution of O3hex.-O3'mon.-P3hex.-P3'mon.-P3''hex. upon Na+ extn. and intercalation is demonstrated to be the key factor to its excellent cycling capability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (NIBs) have attracted great attention for scalable elec. energy storage considering the abundance and wide availability of Na resources.  However, it remains elusive whether carbon anodes can achieve the similar scale of successes in Na-ion batteries as in Li-ion batteries.  Currently, much attention is focused on hard carbon while soft carbon is generally considered a poor choice.  In this study, we discover that soft carbon can be a high-rate anode in NIBs if the prepn. conditions are carefully chosen.  Furthermore, we discover that the turbostratic lattice of soft carbon is electrochem. expandable, where d-spacing rises from 3.6 to 4.2 Å.  Such a scale of lattice expansion only due to the Na-ion insertion was not known for carbon materials.  It is further learned that portions of such lattice expansion are highly reversible, resulting in excellent cycling performance.  Moreover, soft carbon delivers a good capacity at potentials above 0.2 V, which enables an intrinsically dendrite-free anode for NIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref51" class="rightTabRefNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit51" data-doi="10.1016/0022-0248(92)90658-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Möbus, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Karl, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kobayashi, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structure of perylene-tetracarboxylic-dianhydride thin films on alkali halide crystal substrates</span>. <i>J. P and N atoms have been substitutionally doped in graphene networks since the doping takes place at the same time with the graphene growth in the CVD process.  Raman spectra show that the amt. of defects or disorders increases after P and N atoms are incorporated into graphene frameworks.  The doping levels of P and N measured by XPS are 0.6 and 2.6 at %, resp.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Synthesis of phosphorus-doped graphene and its multifunctional applications for oxygen redn. reaction and lithium ion batteries are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref55" class="rightTabRefNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit55" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702781"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amine, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insights into the Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Mechanism of Phosphorus-Functionalized Hard Carbon as Ultrahigh Capacity Anodes</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1702781</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201702781</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref56" class="rightTabRefNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit56" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201600565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reactive Oxygen-Doped 3D Interdigital Carbonaceous Materials for Li and Na Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2783</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2791</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/smll.201600565</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reactive Oxygen-Doped 3D Interdigital Carbonaceous Materials for Li- and Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Ling; Lu, Bingan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2783-2791</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMALBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1613-6810</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbonaceous materials as anodes usually exhibit low capacity for Li ion batteries (LIBs) and Na ion batteries (SIBs).  O-doped carbonaceous materials have the potential of high capacity and super rate performance.  However the reported O-doped carbonaceous materials usually exhibit inferior electrochem. performance.  To overcome this problem, a high reactive O-doped 3-dimensional interdigital porous carbonaceous material is designed and synthesized through epitaxial growth method and used as anodes for LIBs and SIBs.  It delivers high reversible capacity, super rate performance, and long cycling stability (473 mA h g-1 after 500 cycles for LIBs and 223 mA h g-1 after 1200 cycles for SIBs, resp., at the c.d. of 1000 mA g-1), with a capacity decay of 0.0214% per cycle for LIBs and 0.0155% per cycle for SIBs.  Constructing 3-dimensional interdigital porous structure with reactive O functional groups can significantly enhance the electrochem. performance of O-doped carbonaceous material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i9">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b03648" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b03648</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM and TEM morphology, HRTEM morphology, XRD pattern, Raman and XPS analyses, nitrogen sorption and pore diameter analysis, DOS results, CV curves, discharge/charge profiles, Nyquist plots, cycle performance, summary of some reported carbonaceous anode materials for SIBs, and summary of the dual carbonaceous electrode for NDIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03648/suppl_file/am8b03648_si_001.pdf">am8b03648_si_001.pdf (3.89 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

    //Top nav tracking

    (function(){
        $('.header_my-activity a:first').on('click', function(e){
            e.preventDefault();
            //console.log('Button , Activity')

<script nonce="931888aaea3654b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="931888aaea3654b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

  <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931888aaea3654b8-SJC">
    "use strict";

    /*

    var infoCentralCapture = (function () {

      window.mobilecheck = function () {

          if (
            /(android|bb\d+|meego).+mobile|avantgo|bada\/|blackberry|blazer|compal|elaine|fennec|hiptop|iemobile|ip(hone|od)|iris|kindle|lge |maemo|midp|mmp|mobile.+firefox|netfront|opera m(ob|in)i|palm( os)?|phone|p(ixi|re)\/|plucker|pocket|psp|series(4|6)0|symbian|treo|up\.(browser|link)|vodafone|wap|windows ce|xda|xiino/i.test(

        return check;
      };

      var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
      if (_isMobile) {
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").addClass("mobile");
      }

      var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage =
          "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();
          });

          // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
          // $(document).click(function(event) {
          // 	event.stopPropagation();
          //   console.log(event.target);
          //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
          //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
          //     $(this).off();
          //   }
          // });
          $("#gdpr-con-btn").click(function () {
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            hideForm();
          });
        }, time);
      }

      //Show the message
      function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

    $(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
  </script>
</p>
-->

var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
